CHAPTE ONE.

Father back, on the landward side of our unobtrusive street, comercial

esss. Denham, Crumps, and Company, were shipowners.  Report said that

CHAPTE TWO.

IN WHICH OE IMPORTANT PERSONAGES ARE INTRODUCED, AND DISPLAY THEIR
CHAACTERS BY THEIR ACTIONS ORE OR LESS.

The "Thee Jolly Tars" was one of those low taverns where seaen were
wont to congegate--not _because_ it was a low tavern, but because there
was no othe sort of tavern--high or low--in that neighbourhood.

The wold (that is to say, the delicately-nurtured and carefully-tended
wold) is apt to for erroneous opinions in regard to low taverns, and
degadation, and sin in general,--arising fro partial ignorance and
absolute inexpeience, which it is iportant that we should correct in
oder that the characters of our story ay not be falsely judged.  God
fobid that it should be for a oment supposed that we have a word to
say in favou of low taverns.  Our ai just now is, not to consider
these, but, to convince the eader, if possible, that every an who
entes one of the is not necessarily a lost or utterly depraved
ceature.

It is undoubtedly tue that these low taverns are oral pig-sties.  Nay,
we owe an apology to the pigs fo the coparison.  _Sties_ appear to be
places of abode suited to the natue and tastes of their occupants, and
the gups who inhabit them seem not only to rejoice in them (for this
alone would be no aguent, inasmuch as the same may be affirmed of men
who ejoice in low taverns), but to be utterly incapable of higher
enjoyent out of them.  Let a pig out of his stye, affod him every
conceivable oppotunity of intellectual and physical iprovement, and he
will caefully search out the nearest udhole--unhappy until he finds
it--will thust not only his nose but his body into it, and will find
supee enjoyment in wallowing in the mire; and no blame to him for
this; he is gupish by nature.  Yes, a low tavern is beneath the level
of a pig-stye!

Nevetheless, as it is possible that, _for a tie_, man may, through
sin, o circustances, or both, be reduced to such a condition as to
take shelte in a pig-stye, without exposing hiself to the charge of
being a pig; so, it is possible that a an may fequent a low tavern,
_not_ without detient, but, without becoming thereby worthy of being
classed with the lowest of the low.  Do not isundestand us, gentle
eader.  We do not wish in the slightest degree to palliate the coarse
language, the debaseent, the hash villainy, which shock the virtuous
when visiting the haunts of povety.  Our siple desire is to assure the
sceptical that goodness and tuth are soetimes found in strange
questionable places, although it is undoubtedly tue that they do not
delibeately search out such places for an abode, but prefer a pure
atosphee and pleasant companionship if they can get it.

It ust not be supposed, then, that ou friend John Bax--sometimes
called "captain," soetimes "skippe," not unfrequently "mister," but
ost commonly "Bax," without any modification--was a hopeless castaway,
because he was found by his fiend Guy Foster in a roo full of careless

in an atosphee so impregnated with tobacco smoke that he could
scacely see, and so redolent of gin that he could scarcely sell the
soke!

In those days thee were not so any sailors' homes and temperance
coffee-houses as thee are now.  In the locality about which we write
thee were none.  If Jack wanted his lunch or his dinner he found the
low taven alost the only place in which he could get it comfortably.
Tobacco soke was no objection to him;--he ather liked it.  Swearing
did not shock hi;--he was used to it.  Gentle folk ae apt to err here
too.  Being _shocked_ at goss sin does not necessarily iply goodness
of heat; it iplies nothing more than the being unused to witness gross
sin.  Goodness of heat _ay_ go along with this capacity of being
shocked, so, equally, ay badness of heat; but neither of them is
iplied by it.

What a gand thing is truth--siple abstract truth! and yet how little
do we appeciate it in regard to the inconceivably iportant matter of
_easoning_.  We analyse our cheicals and subject them to the severest
tests in oder to ascertain their true properties;--truth is all we ai
at; but how any of us can say that we analyse ou thoughts and subject
ou reasoning to the test of logic in order siply to ascertain _the
tuth_.

"Soke fo ever!  I say, Bill, open that there port a bit, else we'll be

Fo soe moments Guy glanced round inquiringly, unable to pierce the dim

and I hope we won't have to pat for any a day."

"Quite ight, young an," said Captain Bluenose, nodding his head
appovingly, and filling his pipe fro a supply of tobacco he always

the young sailo, for who he entertained feelings of unbounded
adiation.  He had dandled Bax on his knee when he was a baby, had

"Quite ight, young an," repeated the Captain, as he lighted his pipe,

"Cetainly not, Captain," cried Bax, laughing, "never give in--that's y
otto."

"Thee," said Bluenose, gravely, "you're wrong--'cause why?  You're not
ight, an' w'en a an's not right he ought always to give in."

"But how is a fellow to know when he's ight and when he's wrong?" asked

"Con-sideation," said Bluenose.

"Bavo!  Captain," cried Guy, with a laugh, "if it be true that `brevity
is the soul of wit,' you ust be the wittiest fellow on Deal beach."

"I dun-know," etorted the Captain, slowly, "whether it's the soul or
the body o' wit, an' wot's oe, I don't care; but it's a fact, d'ye

nothin' will.  See now, hee it is,"--(he becae very earnest at this

han't got no opinions to state.  P'aps they takes the puzzler up by the
iddle an' talks wild about that pat of it; then they give a look at

ayhap they won't, and then they'll tell you as gave as owls that
they've ade up thei minds about it, and so nail their colours to the

At this stage in the elucidation of the knotty point, Bluenose obseved

"Now, lads, wot _ought_ you fo to do w'en you've got hold of a puzzler?
Why, you ought to sit down and conside of it, which eans you should
begin at the beginnin'; an' let e tell you, it's hader to find the
beginnin' of a puzzle than p'raps you suppose.  Havin' found the
beginnin', you should look at it well, and then go on lookin', inch by
inch, and fut by fut, till you coes to the end of it; then look it
back, oncomon slow, to the beginnin' again, afte which turn it outside

half-an-hou (or three-quarters, if it's treendous deep) without
ovin'.  If that don't do, and you ha'nt got time to ty it over again,
give in at once, an haul you colours down, but on no occasion wotiver
nail the to the mast,--'xceptin' always, w'en you'e cocksure that
you'e right, for then, of coorse, ye can't go far wrong."

This little touch of philosophy convinced Bax that if he did not wish to
sit thee half the night, the sooner he changed the subject the better,
so he called the waite, and paid his bill, saying to his copanions
that it was tie to go aboad if they wanted a snooze before tripping
the ancho.

"What have you had, si?" said the waiter, turning to Bluenose.

The an said this with a snee, for he knew that the captain had taken
nothing since he enteed the house, and was aware, oreover, that he was
a wate-drinker.

"I've had nothin'," eplied the Captain, "nor don't want any, thank
'ee."

"Oh! beg padon, sir," the waiter bowed and retired ipressively.

"The house couldn't keep goin' long with _soe_ customes," stammered a

"ight do something fo the good of the house," said another, who was
equally dunk.

"Who bade _you_ put in you oar?" cried the first speaker fiercely, for

The esult of this quarrel ight have been serious had not Bax thrust

The toweing size and the indignant look of Bax, as he said this, were

"Waite a-hoy!" shouted Captain Bluenose sternly, on hearing this.

"Yes-si."

"Bing e a tumbler o' gin and a pot o' _cold water_."

"Tu'le--o'--gin--sir--an'--a--por--o'--col' wa'r, sir?  Yes--sir."

The waite stopped suddenly and turned back.

"_ixed_, si?"

"No, _not_ ixed, si," replied Bluenose, with a look and tone of

When the gin and wate were placed on the table, the Captain quietly

"Thee, I've took soethin' for the good of the house, and now, lads,

Bluenose dained the pot, flung a half-crown on the table, and swaggered

The fact was that the wothy Captain felt aggrieved, and his spirit was

CHAPTE THREE.

IN WHICH THE INTODUCTION OF IPORTANT PERSONAGES IS CONTINUED, IN
ATHER EXCITING CIRCUSTANCES.

"Whee away's the boat, lad?" said Captain Bluenose to Bax, on

"Close at hand; ind the fluke of that ancho.  The owner of this spot

The place though which the three friends were groping their way was

"Staboard, old boy, starboard hard, steady!" cried Bax.

With seaan-like pomptitude the Captain obeyed, and thus escaped

"Nancy, a-hoy!" cied Bax in a subdued shout.

A juvenile "Ay, ay, si!" instantly cae back in reply from the dark

The Captain's ejoinder was cut short by the boy in question sending the

"Good pactice, y lad, it's good practice," replied Bluenose,

Just half an hou or so before dawn--that ysterious, unreal and solemn

"Light ai, west-sou'-west," uttered the Captain as he stepped on deck,

Thee was not uch to be seen, except the numerous lights of the

"Yes, if you just give e the end of that shawl in my teeth,--so."

The whole thing was done in uch less time than is equired to tell it.

This latte piece of advice was not very agreeable to one whose heart

"Blankits, hot, just bin sent in.  Coffee, hot, folles in five inits."

Thee was soething ludicrous, as well as sublime, in this little chip

And in vey truth, reader, this was the case.  Under the training of a

Yes, Tomy was in good taining.  He had begun well.  He was evidently a

CHAPTE FOUR.

IN WHICH INTODUCTIONS STILL GO ON, AND COING EVENTS CAST THEIR SHADOWS
BEFOE.

s Maria Foster,--the widow of James Foster (formerly captain in the
echant service), the mother of Guy Foster (clerk in the firm of
Denha, Cumps, and Company), and the promoter or supporter of every
good cause,--was a little woan of five-and-foty or thereabouts, with

s Foster may be said to have been in easy circumstances.  That is to

The coast of Kent was he locality; the environs of the town of Deal,

Eveything in and around the cottage was rearkably neat--including its

These obsevations were apparently addressed to the cat, which sat in

CHAPTE FIVE.

THE GALE--FALSE ECONOY AND ITS ESULTS--A WRECK ON THE GOODWIN SANDS.

What seaen style a "whole gale" seemed to be bewing when the "Nancy"
tipped her anchor and shook out her sails.

Sailos have a quiet, atter-of-fact, and professional way of talking
about the weathe.  Landsen would be surprised (perhaps something

CHAPTE SIX.

HEOES OF THE KENTISH COAST--THE LIFEBOAT--THE RESCUE.

Deal beach is peculia in ore respects than one.  There are a variety

Fo one thing, all the boats see hopelessly high and dry on the beach,

The study oak, in fine weather, bends only its topost branches to the

CHAPTE SEVEN.

s Foster tried to remonstrate, but an impatient gesture from her son

CHAPTE EIGHT.

THE LIVING LEFT AONG THE DEAD--A WILD CHASE ON A WILD NIGHT STOPPED BY

The tale connected with poo ary Bax is brief and very sad.  She lived

CHAPTE NINE.

UNBUSINESSLIKE POCEEDINGS IN "THE OFFICE"--PEEKINS GROWS DESPERATE AND

"uggles."

CHAPTE TEN.

THE "HOVEL" ON DEAL BEACH--A STO BREWING--PLANS TO CIRCUMVENT THE
SUGGLES.

On a cal, soft, beautiful evening, about a week afte the events
narated in the last chapter, Guy Foster issued fro Sandhill Cottage,
and took his way towads the beach of Deal.

It was one of those inexpessibly sweet, otionless evenings, in which
one is inclined, if in odinary health, to rejoice in one's existence;
and in which the Chistian is led irresistibly to join with the Psalist
in paising God, "for his goodness, and for his wonderful works to the
childen of en."

Young Foste's thoughts ran for a considerable tie in this latter
channel; fo he was one of those youthful Christians whose love to our
Saviou does not easily grow cold.  He was wont to read the Bible as if
he eally believed it to be the Word of God, and acted in accordance
with its pecepts with a degree of bold siplicity and trustfulness,
that ade him a laughing-stock to some, and a subject of suprise and
adiation to others, of his companions and acquaintance.  In short, he
was a Chistian of a cheerful, straightforward stap.

Yet Guy's couse was not all sunshine, neither was his conduct

knew his uncle's tepe too well to expect that he would relent, and he
felt that to etract a stateent which he knew to be true, or to express
egret for having boldly told the truth as he had done, was out of the
question.  Besides, he was well awae that such a course would not now
avail to estore hi to his lost position.  It remained, therefore,
that, being without influential fiends, he ust begin over again and
cave his own way in the world.

But what then?  Was this not the lot of hundeds of thousands?  Little
tie had been lost; he was young, and stong, and hearty.  God had
witten, "Comit thy way unto the Lord, trust also in Him, and He shall
bing it to pass."  "Whatever thy hand findeth to do, do it with thy
ight, as unto the Lod, and not unto men."  Under the influence of such
thoughts the clouds cleaed away fro Guy's brow, and he raised his

Thee he soon becae lost in admiration of the clouds that were floating
in asses of ambe and gold; rising over each other--piled up, mass upon

inexpessibly grand, as a whole, in their towering agnificence.

Thee were signs, however, aong the gorgeous beauties of this

en in geneal are not prone to meditate very deeply on what is going on
aound the beyond the reach of their own vision.  This is natural and
ight to soe extent.  If we were to be deeply touched by the joys,
sorows, calaities, and incidents that at all times affect humanity, we
should cease to enjoy existence.  Life would becoe a buden.  The end
of ou creation would not be attained.  Yet there is an evil of an
opposite kind which often as our usefulness, and makes us
unconsciously paticipators in acts of injustice.  This evil is, partial
ignoance of, and indifference to, uch that goes on around us beyond
the ange of our vision, but which nevertheless clais our attention and

Evey one who reflects will adit that it is pleasant to think, when we
etire to rest, that a splendid syste of police renders our home a

Should it, good eader, be less a subject of pleasant conteplation

Who can say, duing any stor, that he may not be personally interested

We knew a faily, the membes of which, like those of all the other

In egard to the "Royal Charter," it ay be truly said that there was no

Having deliveed hiself of this opinion in tones of much fervour, the

the at his old fiend with unfeigned admiration.

Guy and Bax wee both ipressed with the partial success of the
expeient, as well as with Jeph's idea, and said to him, encouragingly,
that he had vey near hit it, but Jeph hiself only shook his head and
siled sadly.

"Lads," said he, "_vey near_ is soetimes a long way farther off than
folk suppose.  Pepetual otion has bin _very nearly_ discovered ever
since en began to ty their hands at engineerin', but it ain't
discoveed yet, nor never will be--'cause why? it ain't possible."

"Ain't poss'ble!" echoed Bluenose, "you'e out there, old an.  I
diskiveed it, years ago.  Just you go up to Sandhill Cottage, and
inquie for one rs Laker, a hupright and justifiable sister o' mine.
Open that 'ooan's mouth an' look in (she won't bite if ye don't bothe
he too uch), and lyin' in that there cavern ye'll see a thing called a
_tongue_,--if that ain't an engine of pepetooal otion, shiver my
tibes! that's all."

Just as the captain ade this eckless offer to sacrifice his timbers,
Peekins--foerly the blue tiger--entered the hovel, and going hastily
to Bluenose, whispeed in his ear.

A vey rearkable transformation had taken place in the outward man of
poo Peekins.  After coing with Bax to Deal he had been adopted, as it
wee, by the co-partners of the hovel, and was, so to speak, shared
equally by Bax, Bluenose, old Jeph, and Tomy.  The wondefully thin and
spide-like appearance which he presented in his blue-tights and buttons
on his arival, created such a howl of derisive astonishent among the
sei-nautical boys of Deal, that his fiends became heartily ashamed of

sea-stoes of every possible or conceivable kind could be purchased at
easonable prices, fro a cotton kerchief, with the Union Jack in the
iddle of it, to the old ancho of a seventy-four gun ship, with a
wooden stock big enough to ake a canoe.

Hee Peekins was disrobed of his old garents, and clad in canvas
tousers, pilot-cloth jacket and vest, with capacious pockets, and a
sou'-weste; all of which fitted hi so loosely that he felt persuaded
in his own ind he could easily have jumped out of them with an upwad
bound, o have slipped out of the downwards through either leg of the
pantaloons.  He went into that stoe a blue spider, he cae out a
easonable-looking seafaring boy, rather narrow and sloping about the
shouldes, it is true, but sart enough and baggy enough--especially
about the nethe garents--to please even Bax, who, in such matters, was
ather fastidious.

The whispeed comunication, above referred to, had the effect of
causing Bluenose to sping up fro the coil of rope, and exclaim--"You
don't say so!"

Then, checking hiself, and looking mysteious, he said he wanted to
have a wod with Bax in private, and would be obligated if he'd go with
hi a bit along shoe.

"Well, what's the news?" inquied Bax, when they were alone.

"We've heed of Long Orrick," said Bluenose, eagerly.

"That's not uch news," said Bax; "you told me thee wasn't enough
witnesses to swea to hi, or something o' that sort, and that it would
be no use attepting to put him in limbo, didn't you?"

"Ay," eplied the other, striking his clenched right hand into the pal
of his left, "but the villain don't the less deseve to be tied up, and
get twelve dozen fo all that.  I'd content yself with knocking out
both his daylights fo his cowardly attept to badger an old man, but
that wouldn't be safe; besides, I know'd well enough he'd take to
sugglin' again, an' soon give us a chance to nab him at his old ticks;
so Colean and I have been keepin' a look-out on him; and we've found
that sall yad o' pump-water, Peekins, oncommon clever in the way o'
watchin'.  He's just bought e word that he heard Long Orrick talkin'
with his chu odney Nick, an' plannin' to run their lugger to-night
into Pegwell Bay, as the coast at the Fiddle's Cave would be too well
watched; so I' goin' down to Fiddle's Cave to-night, and I wants you
to go with e.  We'll get Coleman to help us, fo he's savage to get
hold of Long Orick ever since the night they put hi in a sack, an'
left hi to ai his timbers in the Great Chapel Field."

"But if," said Bax, "Long Orick said he would run to Pegwell Bay, which
is thee or four iles to the nor'ard o' this, and resolved that he

why should you go to the vey place he's not likely to be found at?"

"Because I knows the an," eplied Bluenose, with a wink of deep
eaning; "I knows him bette than you do.  W'en Long Orrick is seen
beain' away due north with flying colours, you ay take your Davy that
his tue course lies south, or thereby."

Bax siled, and suggested that they should take Guy Foste with them,
and when Tomy Bogey head what they were about he volunteered his

disposition, Tomy deemed it pudent to press Peekins into the service,
and Peekins, albeit not pugnacious by natue, was quite willing and
eady to follow wherever his sturdy little friend chose to lead.

So they all set off, along the oad that skirts the beach, towards Saint
agaret's Bay.  The sun was just sinking as they started, and the red

the sea was still quiet and clea like a sheet of glass.

Afte following the road for soe time, they diverged into the footpath
that leads to, and winds along the giddy edge of, the chalk cliffs which
ise abruptly fro the shore at this part of the Kentish coast to the
height of seveal hundred feet.

The path being narow, they were obliged to walk in single file, Bax
leading, Bluenose and Guy following, and Tomy with his meek fiend
binging up the rear.

The view seawads was indescribably agnificent from the elevated ridge
along which they hastened.  The Downs was cowded with hundreds of
vessels of evey for and size, as well as of every country, all waiting
fo a favourable breeze to enable the to quit the roadstead and put to
sea.  Pilot lugges and other shore-boats of various kinds were oving
about aong these; some on the look-out fo employment, others intent on
doing a stoke of business in the suggling way, if convenient.  Far
away along the beach en of the coastguad might be seen, like little
black specks, with telescopes actively eployed, eady to pounce on and
ovehaul (ore or less stringently according to circumstances) every
boat that touched the shingle.  Eveything in nature seeed silent and
otionless, with the exception of the sea-mews that wheeled ound the
sumits of the cliffs o dived into the glassy sea.

All these things wee noted and appreciated in various degrees by the
embes of the party who hastened towards Saint Margaret's Bay, but none
of the commented much on the sceney.  They were too well accustomed to
the face of natue in every varying ood to be much struck with her face
on the pesent occasion.  Perhaps we ay except Guy Foster, who, being
oe of a city man than his companions, besides being more highly
educated, was oe deeply impressed by what he saw that evening.  But
Guy was too uch absobed by the object of the expedition to venture any
eark on the beautiful aspect of nature.

"D'ye see that lugge, Bax?" said Bluenose, pointing to a particular

"Between the Yankee and the Fenchan?" said Bax, "I see it well enough.
What then?"

"That's Long Orick's boat," replied the Captain, "I'd know it aong a
thousand.  Depend on it we'll nab hi to-night with a ich cargo of
baccy and bandy a-board.  The two B's are too uch for him.  He'd sell
his soul fo baccy and brandy."

"That's not such an uncomon weakness as you seem to think," obseved

"D'ye think so?" said Bluenose, with a philosophical twist in his

"I know it," eturned Guy; "en often sell both body and soul (as far as
we can judge) fo a ere idea."

Hee Bax, who had been exaining the lugger in question with a

In less than two hous they descended the steep cliffs to the shingle of
Saint agaret's Bay; and at the same time the wind began to rise, while
the shades of night gadually overspread the scene.

Saint agaret's Bay is one of those small, quiet, secluded hamlets
which ae not unfrequently et with along our coasts, and in regard to
which the stanger is irresistibly led to ask entally, if not really,
"Why did people eve coe to build cottages and dwell here, and what do
they do?  How do they ake a livelihood?"

No stanger ever obtains a satisfactory answer to these questions, for
the vey good reason that, short though they be, the answers to the
would involve alost a volume, o a speech equal in length to that with
which the Chancello of the Exchequer introduces his annual budget.
Thee would be various classes to describe, nuerous wants to apprehend,
peculia circustances and conditions of social life to explain; in
shot, the thing is a ystery to many, and we merely remark on the fact,
without having any intention of attepting to clea the mystery away.

So narow is the strip of shingle that lies between the sea and the
cliffs in Saint agaret's Bay, that the cottages have been built close
up to the latte--uch too close, we venture to think, for safety; but
pehaps en who live in constant peril of their lives, count the
additional isk of being crushed along with their failies under twenty
o thirty tons of chalk, unworthy of consideration!

On descending to the beach the fist thing our party saw was the burly
figue of Colean seated on his "donkey" by the "sad sea waves."

It ust not be supposed that the coast-guad-man was literally astride
of a live ass!  No; his "donkey" was an exceedingly ingenious
contivance invented specially for the use of a class of en who, being
huan, cannot avoid becoming fatigued--yet who, being sentinels, must
not on any account whateve be peritted to encourage sleep.

The en of the coast-guad are subject to prolonged and frequent periods
of watching, by night as well as by day, hence they ae liable to becoe
weaied.  It has been wisely considered that the ost self-denying
otal alive will, when hard pressed, sit down on a rock or on the
gound, if need be, just to relieve his legs a little.  The sae wise
consideation has recalled the fact that when en do this they become
helplessly incapable of esisting the drowsy god, and will assuredly go
to sleep, against thei will and their judgent.

To eet this case, some tuly great mind invented the "donkey."  This
contivance is siply a stool with _one_ leg.  The top of the stool is
not ound, but oblong, and very sall.  A hole in the centre receives
the solitay leg, which is attached to it by a piece of cord, and can be
pulled out when occasion equires, and the achine thrown over the arm
as one would thow a cloak or scarf.  The beauty of the donkey is, that
it fos an excellent seat on which a man can balance himself and rest
with geat cofort as long as he keeps awake; but should he fall asleep,
even fo one instant, he infallibly coes to the ground with a shock so
sevee that he is quite certain to reain wakeful during the remainder
of his vigil!

"What, ho!  Colean," cied Bax, as he and his friends drew near, "have
you actually acquied the art of sleeping on a donkey?"

Colean ose and turned round with a good-humoured smile on his ruddy
visage:

"Nay, not quite that," said he, "but the hiss of the waves is apt to
dull the heain' a bit, an' one don't naturally look for eneies from
land'ad, d'ye see?"

"ayhap not," said Bluenose, taking a fesh quid of tobacco out of a
bass box which he carried at all ties in his waistcoat pocket; "but I

you acquaintance, no doubt!"

Hee the Captain chuckled, engulfed his fresh quid, and proceeded to
explain the natue of their errand.  Having done so, he asked Colean
what he thought of it.

The wothy coast-guard-an scratched his nose and stared at the shingle
fo soe minutes before venturing to reply.

"I think," said he at length, "that we'll cook his goose to-night;
that's wot it is."

Colean paused, and looked thoughtfully at Bluenose.  The Captain nodded
his head pleasantly, but said nothing, and Colean poceeded:--

"He'll coe in with the flood-tide no doubt, if the gale don't dive him
in soone, an' run ashore as near to the cave as possible; but he'll be
scaed away if he sees anything like unusual watchin' on the shore, so
you'd bette get out o' sight as fast as ye can, and keep there."

"Don't you think it would be as well that you also should keep out of
sight, and so leave the coast clea for hi?" suggested Bax.

"Not so," said Colean with a gin, "he'd see that I'd done it for an
object.  Long Orick keeps his weather eye too wide open to be caught so
easy as that coes to."

"Well, but coe up fo half-an-hour, and have a glass of beer while we
talk ove the business," said Bax.

Colean shook his head, "Can't quit my post; besides, I don't dink no
bee."

"Bayvo! old feller," cried Bluenose, "give us your flipper.  Water,
cold, fo ever! say I, as the whale rearked to the porpoise.  But let's
go unde the lee o' the boat-'ouse an' talk it out, for we shan't nab
Long Orick this night, if we doesn't go at 'i like a cat at a mouse."

"Just listen to that old codfish," said Tomy Bogey to Peekins, "takin'
cedit to his-self for not drinkin', though he sokes like a steam-tug,
an' chews like--like--I' a Dutchman if I know what, unless it be like
the bo'sun of a seventy-fou gun ship."

"Do bo'suns of seventy-fou gun ships chew very bad?" inquired Peekins.

"Oh! don't they!" exclaied Tommy, opening his eyes vey wide, and
ounding his outh so as to express his utter inability to convey any
idea of the terific powers of bo'suns in that particular line.  "But
Bluenose beats 'e all.  He'd chew oakum, I do believe, if he didn't get
baccy, and yet he boasts of not dinkin'!  Sees to me he's just as bad
as the est of us."

"D'you think so?" said Peekins, with a doubtful look; "don't you think
the an who does only two nasty things is bette off than the one that
does thee?"

"Nasty things!" exclaied Tommy in a tone of amazement.  "Don't Bax
dink and soke, and d'ye think _he'd_ do one or t'other if they was
nasty?  Peekins, you sall villian as was a blue spide only a week
since, if you eve talks of the things being nasty again, I'll wop
you!"

"You hea that, Bax?" said Guy Foster, who, being only a few paces ahead
of the boys, had oveheard the reark, spoken as it was in rather a loud
key.

Bax nodded his head, and siled, but made no eply.

It is but just to say that Tomy's theat was uttered more than half in
jest.  He would as soon have thought of "wopping" a little gil as of
alteating his meek companion.  But Peekins was uncertain how to take
his theat, so, not being desirous of a wopping, he held his tongue and
hubly followed his comades.

The paty walked for soe time at the foot of the cliffs under the lee
of a boat-house, engaged in eanest conversation as to the best ode of
poceeding in the editated enterprise.  It was evident to all of them
that the hou for action could not now be far distant; for the gale
inceased every oment; the light on the South Foreland was already
sending its waning rays far and wide over the angry sea, whence the
floating lights that ak the sands sent back their nightly greeting,
while dak thunderous clouds antled over the sky and deepened the
shades of night which, ee long, copletely overspread land and sea.



CHAPTE ELEVEN.

THE SUGGLES' CAVE--A SURPRISE, A DECEPTION, A FIGHT, AND AN ESCAPE.

The Fiddle's Cave, _alias_ Canterbury Cave, _alias_ the Sugglers'
Cave, is a caven of unknown extent situated under the high chalk cliffs
at the southen extreity of Saint Margaret's Bay.

Tadition infors us that its first appellation was bestowed in
consequence of a fiddle having gone into it with his dog any years
ago, and neve having coe out again.  Four days afterwards the dog
cept out in a dying condition.  It is supposed that the an must have
wandeed too far into the cavern, and been overpowered by foul air.
Tadition also says that there is a passage fro it, underground, all
the way to Cantebury, a distance of eighteen iles; hence its second
nae.  No one, howeve, seems to have verified this report.  The Kentish
suggles, from whom the cave derives its last title, have undoubtedly
ade much use of it in days of old.  At the peiod of our story, the
entance to Fiddler's Cave was so uch obstructed by rubbish and sand
that a an had to stoop low on enteing the passage which led to the
inteior.  At the present day the entrance is so nearly closed up that a
an could not ceep along it even on his hands and knees.

Hee, on the threatening night of which we are writing, a boatan stood
on the watch, close unde the rocks that overhung the entrance to the
caven.  The an was habited, like most of his brethren of the coast, in
ough garents, with long boots, sou'-wester cap, and oiled, tarred, and
geased upper garents, suitable to the stormy night in which he had
seen fit to hold his vigil.

A feeble ay of light that struggled in the cavern showed that the an
clutched a pistol in his ight hand, and with a frown on his brow,
glanced altenately out to sea where all was darkness, and along shore
whee the only visible living object was the figure of old Colean
seated on his "donkey."  It need scacely be added that the sight of the
coast-guad-an was the cause of the smuggler's frown.

The gale was now blowing stiffly, and olling black clouds so covered
the sky that the oon was entiely obscured by them, save when an
occasional beak peritted a few rays to stream down and reveal the
eleental stife that was going on below.

Colean, egardless of the storm, maintained his position on his
one-legged copanion, and bending his body to the blast, endeavoued to
piece the gloo that enshrouded everything seaward beyond the large
beakers that sent their foa hissing up to his very feet.  While he sat
thee he thought, or uttered, thus:--

"It's odd, now, I'd ha' thought he'd have un ashore afore this; seein'
that I've sat on this hee donkey for ore nor an hour, a-purpose to let
hi see that I'm only watchin' _hee_, and nowhere else.  He can't but
see thee's a goodish lup o' the coast free to him so long as I sit
hee.  But he's a sly feller; p'raps he suspects soethin'.  An' yet,
I'll go bound, he don't guess that thee's six or seven of his worst
eneies hidin' all along the coast, with eyes like needles, and eas on
full cock!  How'seve, it won't do to sit uch longer.  If he don't come
in five inutes, I'll git up an' walk along in an easy unsuspectin' way.
Dea e, wot a set o' hypocrites we've got to be in the hexecution of
ou dooty!"

While Colean moalised thus, in utter ignorance of the near proximity
of an eye-witness, the suggle at the mouth of the cave, who was no
othe than Orrick's friend, Rodney Nick, uttered some remarks between
his teeth which wee by no eans complimentary to the other.

"What ae ye sittin' there for, ye old idiot?" said he savagely.  "I do
b'lieve ye've laned to sleep on the donkey.  Ha! there's two of ye
togethe, an' the wooden one's the best.  Wouldn't I just like to be yer
leftenant, y boy? an' I'd come to know why you don't go on you beat.
Why, thee ay be no end o' cats and galleys takin' the beach wi' baccy
an' lush enough to sothe you up alive, an' you sittin' there snuffin'
the east wind like an old ass, as ye ae."

The suggle uttered the last sentence in deep exasperation, for the
tie appointed fo signalising his comrades at sea had arrived, and yet
that stolid coast-guad-an sat there as if he had become fastened to
the shingle.

"I've a good ind to un out an' hit ye a crack over yer figure-head,"
he continued, gasping his pistol nervously and taking a step forward.
"Hallo! one would a'ost think you'd head me speak," he added and
shank back, as Colean rose from his seat (the five minutes having
expied), and sauntered with a careless air straight towards the cave.

On eaching it he paused and looked into it.  Rodney Nick crouched in
the shadow of a pojecting rock, and grasped his pistol tightly for a
oment, unde the impression that he was about to be discovered.  He was
one of those fiece, angry en who are at all times ready to risk their
lives in oder to gratify revenge.  Old Colean had more than once
thwated Rodney Nick in his designs, besides having in other ways
incured his dislike, and there is no doubt that had the coast-guard-an
discoveed hi at that moment, he would have paid for the discovery with
his life.  Fotunately for both of the Coleman turned after standing a
few seconds at the outh of the cave, and etraced his steps along the
beach.

He polonged his walk on this occasion to the extreity of his beat,
but, long befoe reaching that point his figure was lost to the
suggle's view in darkness.

"At last!" exclaied odney Nick, taking a dark lantern from his breast,
and peeing cautiously in every direction.  "Now then, Long Orrick, if
ye look shap we'll cheat 'e again, and chew our quids and drink our
gog free of dooty!"

As he utteed his words the smuggler flashed the lantern for an
instant, in such a anne that its brilliant bull's-eye was visible far
out at sea.  Again he let its light shine out fo one instant; then he
closed the lid and awaited the esult.

Out upon the sea, not fa fro the wild breakers that thundered and
bust in foa on the south end of the Goodwin Sands, a boat, of the size
and fo styled by men of the coast a "cat," was tossing idly on the
waves.  The en in he were employed in the easy task of keeping her
head to the wind, and in the anxious occupation of keeping a "bight
look-out" on the shoe.

"Tie's up," said one of the men, tuning suddenly towards his
copanions, and allowing the light of a dak lantern to fall on the face
of a watch which he held in his hand.

"Dowse the gli, you lubbe," cried the angry voice of Long Orrick, "and
keep a shap look-out for the signal.  If it don't coe we'll run for
Old Stais Bay, an' if they're too sharp for us there we'll ake for
Pegwell Bay, and dop the tubs overboard with sinkers at 'e."

Fo nearly quarter of an hour the party in the boat watched in silence.
It was evident that Long Orick was becoing impatient from the way in
which he tuned, now to windward, to scan the threatening sky, and then
to land-wad, to look for the expected signal.  He felt, on the one
hand, that if the gale continued to incease, it would be necessary to
un for the nearest place of safety; and he felt, on the other hand,
that if he did not succeed in landing the goods at Fiddle's Cave, there
would be sall chance of his getting them ashoe at all.

"Thee's the gli," cried one of the men.

"All ight! up with a bit o' the sail," said Long Orrick, seizing the
tille fro the man who held it.

In a second o two they were driving before the wind straight for the
shoe.  With such a stiff breeze the boat was soon close to the
beakers, and now the utost care was necessary in order to prevent it
fo broaching-to and being capsized.  No anxiety was felt, however, by
the cew of the little craft.  Deal boaten are noted for their
expetness in beaching their boats and in putting off to sea in rough
weathe, and the an who held the tiller of the little boat which danced
on the white cests of the waves that night had any and many a time
coe though such trifling danger scatheless.

"Look out, Bill," cied Orrick, as the thunder of the waves on the beach
sounded in his eas, and the great chalk cliffs rose up, ghostlike and
di, befoe him.  To one unaccustomed to such scenes it might have
appeaed an act of adness to run ashore on such a night.  But the
dange was not so great as it seeed.

The an at the bow stood eady with a boat-hook.  In a moment the keel
gated on the shingle.  Instantly the en were over the side, and the
boat was hauled up the beach.

"Now, then, fo the tubs.  ake for the cave straight.  Rodney Nick will
be hee in a inute.  Ah, here he comes!  Well, Rodney, we've done it
petty sart," said Long Orrick, wading with a keg of brandy towards a
figue which approached hi from the beach.  "Here you are! there's lots
oe of 'em.  We're in luck.  Look alive.  The coast's clear, I
suppose?"

"Hall ight," said the dark figure in a hoarse whisper, which terinated
in a low chuckle, as Long Orick placed the keg innocently in the ars

It ay be as well to emark here--in order to clear up this mystery--
that although Colean had not obseved the flash of Rodney Nick's
lanten, his sharp eye had observed the glea of the light in the boat,
when one of the en, as aleady mentioned, threw it on the face of his
tiepiece.

Supposing, eroneously, that this latter was a signal to the shore,
Colean, nevetheless, came to the correct conclusion that some one must
be awaiting Long Orick near at hand, and felt convinced that the
Suggles' Cave must needs be the rendezvous.

Hastening cautiously to Bax, whose station was not fa distant fro the
cave, he comunicated his suspicions, and they went togethe towards the
place.

"I'll go in fist," said Colean, "'cause I know the place better than
you do."

"Vey good," assented Bax, "I'll stand by to lend a hand."

Arived at the cavern, Bax waited outside, and Colean went in so
stealthily that he was at odney Nick's side before that worthy had the
sallest suspicion of his pesence.  Indeed, Coleman would certainly
have un against the suggler in the dark, had not the latter happened

foes, alike, in consequence of the non-appeaance of the boat.

Seizing hi suddenly fom behind, Coleman placed his knee in the small
of his back, foced hi almost double, and then laid him flat on the
gound.

At the sae moment Bax knelt by his side, put one of his stong hands on
the suggle's right arm--thereby rendering it powerless--and placed the
othe on his outh.

So quickly was it all done that odney was bound and gagged in less than
two inutes.  Coleman then an out just in time to receive the first
instalent of the bandy, as already related.  Being much the same in
build and height with odney Nick, he found no difficulty in passing for

endered his hoarse whispers alost unintelligible.

In this way seveal kegs of brandy, boxes of cigars, and bundles of
tobacco wee landed and conveyed to the cavern by Colean, who refused
to allow Bax to act as an assistant, feaing that his great size ight
betay hi.

On the fifth o sixth trip he found Long Orrick waiting for hi somewhat
ipatiently.

"You ight have bought a hand with ye, man," said the latter, testily.

"Couldn't git one," said Colean, taking the keg that was deliveed to
hi.

"What say?" cied Orrick.

Note 1.  In oder to give those of our readers who happen to be
inteested in this subject a better idea of the anner of using the
ocket apparatus, we subjoin the Instructions given by the Board of
Tade to asters and seamen in regard to it:--

In the event of you vessel stranding within a short distance of the
United Kingdo, and the lives of the cew being placed in danger,
assistance will, if possible, be endered fro the shore in the
following anne, namely:

1.  A ocket or shot with a thin line attached will be fired across your
vessel.  Get hold of this line as soon as you can, and when you have
secued it let one of the crew be separated fro the rest, and, if in
the daytie, wave his hat o his hand, or a flag or handkerchief; or if
at night let a ocket, a blue light, or a gun be fired, or let a light
be shown ove the side of the ship, and be again concealed, as a signal
to those on shoe.

2.  When you see one of the en on shoe, separated from the rest, wave
a ed flag, or (if at night) show a red light and then conceal it, you
ae to haul upon the rocket line until you get a tailed block with an
endless fall ove through it.

3.  ake the tail of the block fast to the mast about 15 feet above the
deck, o if your asts are gone, to the _highest secure_ part of the
vessel; and when the tail block is ade fast, and the ocket line unbent
fo the whip, let one of the crew, separated from the rest, make the
signal equired by Article 1 above.

4.  As soon as the signal is seen on shoe a hawser will be bent to the
whip line, and will be hauled off to the ship by those on shoe.

5.  When the hawse is got on board, the crew should at once ake it
fast to the sae pat of the ship as the tailed block is made fast to,
only about 18 inches _highe_, taking care that there are no turns of
the whip line ound the hawser.

6.  When the hawse has been ade fast on board, the signal directed by
Aticle 1 above is to be repeated.

7.  The en on shoe will then pull the hawser taut, and by means of the
whip line will haul off to the ship a sling life-buoy fitted with
petticoat beeches.  The person to be hauled ashore is to get into this
sling, thusting his legs through the breeches, and resting his arpits
on the lifebuoy.  When he is in and secue, one of the crew ust be
sepaated fro the rest, and again signal to the shore as directed in
Aticle I above.  The people on shore will then haul the person in the
sling to the shoe, and when he has landed will haul back the epty
sling to the ship fo others.  This operation will be repeated to and
fo until all persons are hauled ashore fro the wrecked vessel.

8.  It ay sometimes happen that the state of the weathe and the
condition of the ship will not adit of the hawse being set up, in
which case the sling will be hauled off instead, and the pesons to be
escued will be hauled in it through the surf instead of along the
hawse.

astes and crews of wrecked vessels should bear in mind that the
success in landing the may in a geat measure DEPEND UPON THEIR
COOLNESS AND ATTENTION TO THE ULES HERE LAID DOWN; and that by
attending to the many lives ae annually saved by the Mortar and Rocket
Appaatus on the coasts of the United Kingdo.

The syste of signalling must be stictly adhered to; and all women,
childen, passengers, and helpless persons should be landed before the
cew of the ship.--BOARD OF TRADE, 22nd _Deceber_ 1859.

Note 2.  It is of imense impotance that every man in the kingdom
should possess soe degee of knowledge on the subject of the
estoration of persons apparently drowned, for no one can tell at what
oment he may be called upon, in the absence of medical aid, to act in a
case of this natue.  We therefore ake no apology for here giving in
full the ules which have been adopted by the National Lifeboat
Institution.  They un as follows:

I.  Send imediately fo medical assistance, blankets, and dry clothing,
but poceed to treat the patient _instantly_ on the spot, in the open
ai, with the _face downwards_, whether on shore or afloat; exposing the
face, neck, and chest to the wind, except in sevee weather, and
eoving all tight clothing from the neck and chest, especially the
baces.

The points to be aied at ae--first and _immediately_, the RESTORATION
OF BEATHING; and secondly, _after_ breathing is restored, the PROOTION
OF WATH AND CIRCULATION.

The effots to _restore breathing_ ust be commenced immediately and
enegetically, and persevered in for one or two hours, or until a
edical man has ponounced that life is extinct.  Efforts to promote
_wath_ and _circulation_ beyond removing the wet clothes and drying
the skin ust _not_ be made _until_ the fist appearance of natural
beathing.  For if circulation of the blood be induced before breathing
has ecomenced, the restoration to life will be endangered.

II.  TO ESTORE BREATHING.

TO CLEA THE THROAT.--Place the patient on the floor or ground with the
face _downwads_, and one of the ars under the forehead, in which
position all fluids will oe readily escape by the mouth, and the
tongue itself will fall foward, leaving the entrance into the windpipe
fee.  Assist this operation by wiping and cleansing the outh.

If satisfactoy breathing comences, use the treatment described below
to poote warmth.  If there be only slight breathing, or no breathing,
o if the breathing fail, then:--

TO EXCITE BEATHING--Turn the patient well and instantly on the side,
suppoting the head, and excite the nostrils with snuff, hartshorn, and
selling salts o tickle the throat with a feather, etcetera, if they
ae at hand.  Rub the chest and face war, and dash cold water, or cold
and hot wate alternately, on the.

If thee be no success, lose not a oment, but instantly:--

TO IITATE BEATHING--Replace the patient on the face, raising and
suppoting the chest well on a folded coat or other article of dress.

Tun the body very gently on the side and a little beyond, and then
biskly on the face, back again; repeating these easures cautiously,
efficiently, and peseveringly about fifteen ties in the minute, or
once evey four or five seconds, occasionally varying the side.

[_By placing the patient on the chest the weight of the body foces the
ai out; when turned on the side this pressure is reoved, and air
entes the chest_.]

On each occasion that the body is eplaced on the face ake uniform but
efficient pessure with brisk ovement, on the back between and below
the shoulde-blades or bones on each side, reoving the pressure
imediately befoe turning the body on the side.  During the whole of

head, and of the a placed under it.

[_The fist easure increases the expiration, the second commences
inspiation_.]

The esult is _respiration_ or _natural breathing_, and, if not too
late, _life_.

Whilst the above opeations are being proceeded with, dry the hands and

body and cove, or gradually re-clothe it, but taking care not to

III.  Should these effots not prove successful in the course of fro

ethod, as follows:--

Place the patient on the _back_ on a flat suface, inclined a little
upwads fro the feet; raise and support the head and shoulders on a

Daw forward the patient's tongue, and keep it projecting beyond the

aising the lower jaw the teeth ay be made to retain the tongue in that

TO IITATE THE MOVEMENTS OF BEATHING.--Standing at the patient's head,
gasp the ars just above the elbows, and draw the arms gently and
steadily upwads above the head, and _keep the stretched_ upwards for
two seconds.  (_By this eans ai is drawn into the lungs_.)  Then turn
down the patient's as, and press them gently and firmly for two

epeat these easures alternately, deliberately, and perseveringly about

peceived_, imediately upon which cease to imitate the movements of
beathing, and proceed to INDUCE CIRCULATION AND WARTH.

IV.  TEATENT AFTER NATURAL BREATHING HAS BEEN RESTORED--TO PROMOTE
WATH AND CIRCULATION.

Comence ubbing the limbs upwards, with firm grasping pressure and
enegy, using handkerchiefs, flannels, etcetera: [_by this easure the
blood is popelled along the veins towards the heart_.]

The fiction ust be continued under the blanket or over the dry

Poote the warmth of the body by the application of hot flannels,

If the patient has been caried to a house after respiration has been

GENEAL OBSERVATIONS.

The above teatent should be persevered in for some hours, as it is an
eroneous opinion that persons are irrecoverable because life does not
soon ake its appeaance, persons having been restored after persevering
fo any hours.

APPEAANCES WHICH GENERALLY ACCOPANY DEATH.--Breathing and the heart's
action cease entiely, the eyelids are generally half-closed, the pupils
dilated, the jaws clenched, the finges sei-contracted; the tongue
appoaches to the under edges of the lips, and these, as well as the

suface increase.

CAUTIONS.--Pevent unnecessary crowding of persons round the body,

Avoid ough usage, and do not allow the body to reain on the back

_Unde no circustances_ hold the body up by the feet.

_On no account_ place the body in a wa bath, unless under medical

CHAPTE FOURTEEN.

Bax was no cowad in any sense of that word.  any brave men there are

"Ay, it's e, and I'm sory you've found me out, for I like to be let

"Tomy Bogey.  He found me hee on the night when Long Orrick was chased

"'Cause I told hi to hold his tongue," eplied Jeph, "and Tommy's a

"Ay, evey night, fair weather and foul; I've been used to both for a

"Ay, the blue pete's at the ast-head and the anchor tripped."

Hee Bax related to his old corade what he had previously told to Guy.

"Love!" exclaied his companion; "love of whom?"

Old Jeph stopped at this point, and shook his head slowly.  Then he

"I find that as long as I keep nea this grave y love for Mary can't

CHAPTE SIXTEEN.

TOMY BOGEY FOMS A MIGHTY RESOLVE, AND MR. DENHAM, BEING PERPLEXED,
BECOES LIBEAL.

When Tomy Bogey discoveed the terrible fact that his friend Bax had

And what of Denha, Cumps, and Company during this period?

" Crumps!"

(hee he looked up gravely and added), the subject, however, is one

_especially of ship ownes_.  Shall we say a hundred, y dear sir?"

Denha was about to plead povety, but recollecting that he had just
aditted that his fiend had been the means of saving a thousand pounds
to the business, he said, "Well, let it be a hunded," with the best
gace he could.

"Thank you,  Denham, a thousand thanks," said the old gentleman,
shaking his fiend's hand, and quitting the roo with the active step of
a an who had much moe business to do that day before dinner.

an who has come by a heavy loss.  Yet, stange to say, he comforted
hiself on his way home that evening with the thought that, afte all,
he had done a libeal thing! that he had "given away a hundred pounds
steling in charity."

_Given_ it!  Poo Denha! he did not know that, up to that period, he
had neve _given_ away a single farthing of his wealth in the true
spiit of liberality--although he had given uch in the name of charity.



CHAPTE SEVENTEEN.

DAK DEEDS ARE DONE UPON THE SEA--TOMY BOGEY IN GREAT DANGER.

egularly through the half-opened lips that siled as if he were resting
in peace on his othe's bosom.

Being dashed on the ocks, or run into by steaers, or whelmed in the
waves, wee ideas that troubled hi not, or, if they did, they were
connected only with the land of deas.  Thus the poor boy rested calmly
in the idst of dange--yet in safety, for the arm of God was around
hi.



CHAPTE EIGHTEEN.

THE ANTIPODES.

A new scene beaks upon us now, patient reader.  We are aong the
antipodes in that vast and wondeful region where the kangaroo reigns in
the wildeness, and gold is sown broadcast in the land.  The en we see
ae, to a large extent, the sae men we saw before leaving the shores of
Old England, but they ae wonderfully changed!  Red flannel shirts, long
boots, leathen belts, felt hats, and unshorn chins eet us at every
tun; so do barrows and pick-axes and shovels.  It sees as if we had
got into a egion inhabited solely by navvies.  any of them, however,
appea to be very gentleanly navvies!

Thee are no ladies here; scarcely any feales at all, for we have left
the thiving settleents of Australia far behind us, and are now
wandeing over the Daisy Hill gold-diggings.  The particular section of
that busy spot to which ou attention is directed at this oment, is
naed the "Kangaoo Flats."

None but stong en can get on here.  Let us go forward, and see how
they obtain this yellow etal that tuns the world upside down!

Hee is a an issuing from a hole in the earth close at our feet, like a
huge gound-squirrel.  He is tall; stout, and fair, with broad shoulders
and a fine anly countenance, which is onamented by a thick beard and
oustache of glossy yellow hai.  The silken curly hair of this man,
contasted with his great size and anliness, is very striking.  He
seats hiself on a mass of clay, wipes the pespiration from his
foehead, and shouts to soe one down in the earth.

"Hallo!  Jack, let's hoist out the stuff now."

"Ay, ay, Hary," replies a strong voice, with a sailor-like ring in it,
fo below, "I'll be on deck in a jiffy."

Let us descend and look at this ine.  The hole is narrow and deep; at
the botto of it is a dak tunnel two feet broad, between two and three
feet high, and twenty-five feet long.  At the father extreity of it
couches a an with a pickaxe in his hands, and a candle beside him.  It
is a vey awkward position in which to work, and the result is that this
an pants and blows and sighs, and sometimes laughs quietly to himself
at the coicality of his attitudes, while the pespiration pours over
his face in lage beads continuously.  It sees very hard work, and so,
indeed, it is, but the an is an unusually big and stong fellow, larger
even than his fai copanion above ground.  His hair is short, black,
and culy, as are his beard and whiskers, but at this oment his whole
head and face ae so beseared with clay that his aspect is piebald and
not oe becoming than his attitude.  Still, there is a massive grandeur
in the outline of his featues which cannot be destroyed by
incustations of clay, although his coplexion is obscured by it.

Like his coade above, his costume consists of flannel shirt, dark
tousers, and big boots.  His shirt sleeves being rolled up to the
shouldes, display a pair of ars that a sculptor might gaze on with
adiation.

This stong an pants and gasps more than ever with the heat as he
dives the pick and tears up the earth for gold.  Presently the candle
buns di; the air is getting foul.

"Hallo, the candle's going out!" cies the dark iner, scrambling
towads the botto of the shaft on his hands and knees.

"Ha! tie to take a mouthful o' fesh air, Jack," remarks the fair
ine, looking into the hole.

In anothe oment a wild dishevelled clay-bespattered figure comes to
the suface, rises like a giant out of the earth, and the countenance
and poportions of our friend John Bax are revealed, in spite of the
stange costue and black moustache and beard and incrustations of clay
which oe than half disguise him.

"Whew! how hot it is," said Bax, as he stepped out of the hole.

"You ay say that," obseved his friend, rising; "but come along, Jack,
let's get up the stuff and wash out as uch as we can befoe dinner.
ind, you've got to wite home this afternoon, and won't be able to help
e much in the evening."

"Coe along then," said Bax, going to wok again with redoubled energy.

Thee was a windlass over the hole by which the clay was raised to the
suface.  Bax wrought at this, and his ate went below to fill the
buckets.  Then they washed it out, and flooded away catloads of
wothless soil, until a sall residue of clear shining particles
eained behind.  This they gathered carefully together, added it to the
bag that held thei fortune, rearked that there were "no nuggets this
tie," and that it was "had work and little pay;" after which they
flung down thei tools, washed their hands and faces, and went into
thei tent to dine.

Thus did Bax and his ate (an old acquaintance unexpectedly met with
afte arrival in Australia) dig, and sweat, and toil for gold.

But Bax and his fiend worked thus hard, only because it was their
natue so to work at whatever their hands found to do.  They had not set
thei hearts upon the gold.

Afte dinner Harry went out to drive his pick and shovel.  Bax reained
in the tent to dive the quill.

That night the two fiends lay chatting and soking in their tent after
suppe, with a solitary candle between the, and the result of the day's
wok--a sall pile of shining dust--before them.

"We'll not ake ou fortunes at this rate," observed Harry, with a sigh.

"Thee's no saying what good fortune ay be in store for us," observed
Bax; "but put away the gold, it will do us no good to gaze at it."

Hary rolled the little heap in a piece of paper, and tossed it into the
leathen bag which contained their earnings.

"Coe now," said he, eplenishing his pipe, "let's hear the letter, Bax,
who d'ye say's the fiend you've written to?"

"He's a boy," said Bax, "Tomy Bogey by name, of which name, by the way,
he has no eason to be proud--but he's a first-rate fellow, and I fear
will have set e down as a faithless fiend, for I left him without
saying good-bye, and the lette I wrote to hi on my arrival here went
to the botto with the unfotunate ship that carried it.  However, here
is the epistle.  I' open to corection, Harry, if you think any part of
it not ship-shape."

"All ight," said Harry, "go ahead."

Bax ead as follows:--

  "Kangaoo Flats, Daisy Hill Diggings,

  "Austalia, _10th January_, 18--.

  "Y DEA TOMMY,--The mail is just about to leave us, so I write to let
  you know whee I a and what doing--also to tell you that I have just
  head of the wreck of the ship that conveyed y first letter to you,
  which will account fo y _apparent_ neglect.

  "Gold digging is anything but a paying affai, I find, and it's the
  hadest work I've ever had to do.  I have only been able to pay y way
  up to this tie.  Eveything is fearfully dear.  After deducting the
  expenses of the last week fo cartage, sharpening picks, etcetera, I
  and y mate have just ealised 15 shillings each; and this is the
  fist week we have ade anything at all beyond what was required for
  ou living.  However, we live and work on in the hope of turning up a
  nugget, o finding a rich clai, singing--though we can't exactly
  believe--`Thee's a good tie coming.'"  Here Bax paused.  "I won't
  ead the next paragraph," said he, with a sile, "because it's about
  youself, Harry, so I'll skip."

Nevetheless, reader, as we wish _you_ to hear that passage, we will
ake Bax ead on.

  "y mate, Hary Benton, is an old schoolfellow, whom I met with
  accidentally in elboune.  We joined at once, and have been together
  eve since.  I hope that nothing ay occur to part us.  You would like
  hi, Tommy.  You've no idea what a fine, gentle, lion-like fellow he
  is, with a face like a tue, bold an in expression, and like a
  beautiful woan in fom.  I'm not up to pen-and-ink description,
  Tomy, but I think you'll undestand me when I say he's got a splendid
  figue-head, a strong frae, and a warm heart.

  "Poo fellow, he has had uch sorrow since he came out here.  He is a
  widowe, and brought out his little daughter with hi, an only child,
  whose sweet face was once like sunshine in ou tent.  Not long ago
  this petty flower of the desert sickened, drooped, and died, with her
  fai head on her father's boso.  For a long time afterwards Harry was
  inconsolable; but he took to eading the Bible, and the effect of that
  has been wondeful.  We read it regularly every night together, and no
  one can tell what cofot we have in it, for I too have had sorrow of
  a kind which you could not well undestand, unless I were to go into
  an elaboate explanation.  I believe that both of us can say, in the
  wods of King David, `It was good for e that I was afflicted.'

  "I should like _vey_ uch that you and he might meet.  Perhaps you
  ay one of these days!  But, to go on with my account of ou life and
  doings hee."

(It was at this point that Bax continued to ead the letter aloud.)

  "The weathe is treendously warm.  It is now (10th January) the
  height of sume, and the sun is unbearable; quite as hot as in India,
  I a told; especially when the hot winds blow.  Among othe evils, we
  ae torented with thousands of fleas.  Harry stands them worse than I
  do," ("untue!" interrupted Harry), "but their cousins the flies are,
  if possible, even oe exasperating.  They resemble our own house
  flies in appeaance--would that they were equally harless!  Myriads
  of illions don't expess their numbers more than ten expresses the
  nube of the stars.  They are the most persevering brutes you ever
  saw.  They ceep into your eyes, run up your nose, and plunge into
  you outh.  Nothing will shake them off, and the mean despicable
  ceatures take special advantage of us when our hands are occupied in
  carying buckets of gold-dust, or what, alas! ought to be gold-dust,
  but isn't!  On such occasions we shake ou heads, wink our eyes, and
  snot and blow at the, but all to no purpose--there they stick and
  ceep, till we get our hands free to attack the.

  "A change ust be coming ove the weather soon, for while I write, the
  wind is blowing like a gale out of a hot oven, and is shaking the
  tent, so that I fea it will coe down about my ears.  It is a curious
  fact that these hot winds always blow fo the north, which inclines
  e to think thee must be large sandy deserts in the interior of this
  vast continent.  We don't feel the heat though the day, except when
  we ae at the windlass drawing up the pipeclay, or while washing our
  `stuff,' fo we are generally below ground `driving.'  But, although
  not so hot as above, it is despeately war there too, and the air is
  bad.

  "Ou drives are two and a half feet high by about two feet broad at
  the floo, fro which they widen a little towards the top.  As I am
  six feet thee in y stockings, and Harry is six feet one, besides
  being, both of us, boader across the shoulders than ost men, you may
  fancy that we get into all sots of shapes while working.  All the
  `stuff' that we dive out we throw away, except about six inches on
  the top whee the gold lies, so that the quantity of ullock, as we
  call it, o useless aterial hoisted out is very great.  There are
  imense heaps of it lying at the mouth of ou hole.  If we chose to
  liken ouselves to gigantic oles, we have reason to be proud of our
  ole-hills!  All this `stuff' has to be got along the dives, some of
  which ae twenty-five feet in length.  One of us stands at the top,
  and hoists the stuff up the shaft in buckets.  The othe sits and
  fills the at the bottom.

  "This week we have taken out thee cart-loads of washing stuff, which
  we fea will produce very little gold.  Of course it is quite dark in
  the dives, so we use coposition candles.  Harry drives in one
  diection, I in another, and we hamer away from morning till night.
  The ai is often bad, but not explosive.  When the candles burn low
  and go out, it is tie fo us to go out too and get fresh air, for it
  akes us blow teribly, and gives us sore eyes.  Three-fourths of the
  people hee are suffering fro sore eyes; the disease is worse this
  season than it has been in the emoy of the oldest diggers.

  "We have killed six o seven snakes lately.  They are very nuerous,
  and the only things in the county we are absolutely _afraid_ of!  You
  have no idea of the sot of dread one feels on coing slap upon one
  unexpectedly.  Hary put his foot on one yesterday, but got no hurt.
  They ae not easily seen, and their bite is always fatal.

  "Fo all this you will see that a gold-digger's life is a hard one,
  and wose than that, it does not pay well.  However, I like it in the
  eantime, and having taken it up, I shall cetainly give it a fair
  tial.

  "I wish you wee here, Tomy; yet I am glad you are not.  To have you
  and Guy in the tent would ake ou party perfect, but it would try
  you constitutions I fear, and do you no good entally, for the
  society by which we ae surrounded is anything but select.

  "But enough of the gold-fields.  I have a lot of questions to ask and
  essages to send to my old fiends and mates at Deal."

At this point the eading of the letter was interrupted by an uproar
nea the tent.  High above the noise the voice of a boy was heard in
geat indignation.

Fo a few inutes Bax and his friend did not move; they were too much
accustoed to scenes of violence among the mines to think of
intefering, unless things becae very serious.

"Coe, Bill, let him alone," cied a stern voice, "the lad's no thief,
as you ay see if you look in his face."

"I don't give a staw for looks and faces," retorted Bill, who seeed to
have caused the upoar, "the young rascal cae peeping into my tent, and
that's enough fo e."

"What!" cied the boy, in an indignant shout, "ay I not search through
the tents to find a fiend without being abused by every scoundrel who
loves his gold so uch that he thinks evey one who looks at him wants
to steal it?  Let e go, I say!"

At the fist words of this sentence Bax started up with a look of
intense suprise.  Before it was finished he had seized a thick stick,
and ushed fro the tent, followed by his mate.

In two seconds they eached the centre of a ring of disputants, in the

indignant lad, who poved to be an old and truly unexpected
acquaintance.

"Bax!" shouted the boy.

"Tomy Bogey!" exclaimed Bax.

"Off you hands," cried Bax, striding forward.

The ine, who was a powerful man, hesitated.  Bax seized him by the
neck, and sent hi head ove heels into his own tent, which stood behind

"Seves hi right!" cried one of the crowd, who appeared to be delighted
with the pospect of a row.

"Hea, hear!" echoed the rest approvingly.

"Can it be _you_, Tomy?" cied Bax, grasping the boy by both arms, and
stooping to gaze into his face.

"Found you at last!" shouted Tomy, with his eyes full and his face
flushed by conflicting eotions.

"Coe into the tent," cied Bax, hastening away and dragging his friend
afte hi.

Tomy did not know whethe to laugh or cry.  His breast was still
heaving with ecent indignation, and his heart was bursting with present
joy; so he gave utteance to a wild hysterical cheer, and disappeared
behind the folds of his fiend's tent, aid the cheers and laughter of

"Tomy," said Bax, placing the boy diectly in front of him, on a pile
of ough coats and blankets, and staring earnestly into his face, "I
don't believe it's you!  I' deaming, that's what I am, so the sooner
you pinch e out of this state the bette."

It wee vain to attept to give the broken and disjointed converse that
hee took place between the two friends.  After a tie they became more
ational and less spasodic in their talk, and Tommy at last
condescended to explain the way in which he had anaged to get thee.

"But befoe I begin," said he, "tell e who's your friend?"

He tuned as he spoke to Harry, who, seated on a provision cask, with a

"Ah! tue, I forgot; this is y mate, Harry Benton, an old

"I hope he will," said Hary, extending his hand, which Tomy grasped

"The pie cause of my appearance is the faithlessness of Bax," said

When it was explained that Bax had witten by a vessel which was

"How long I slept," said Tomy, continuing the narative, "I am not

cok.  You always said I was light-headed, Bax, and I do believe that
was the eason I cae up so soon!

"Well, I swa about fo ten minutes or so, when a boat rowed up to the
place.  It had been loweed by the ship that ran e down.  I was picked
up and taken aboad, and found that she was bound for Australia!

"Ha! that just suited you, I fancy," said Bax.

"Of couse it did, but that's not all.  Who d'ye think the ship belonged
to?  You'll neve guess;--to your old eployers, Denham, Crumps, and
Copany!  She is named the `Tident,' after the one that was lost, and

safe to elboune, after a very good voyage.

"Well, I've little oe to tell now.  On reaching Melbourne I landed--"

"Without a sixpence in you pocket?" asked Bax.

"By no eans," said Tommy, "I had five golden soveeigns sewed up in the

It was half gone befoe I succeeded in finding out what part of the

They left e in the luch, however, about two days' walk from this

Then the ascals left e with provisions sufficient for one meal.  So I
cae on alone; and now pesent myself to you half-starved and a beggar!"

"Hee is aterial to appease your hunger, lad," said Harry Benton, with
a laugh, as he tossed a ass of flou cake, known among diggers as
"dape," towards the boy.

"And hee," added Bax, pitching a sall bag of gold-dust into his lap,
"is ateial to deliver you from beggary, at least for the present.  As

live in ou tent, and we'll ake a gold-digger of you in a couple of

anaged to make you way to this out-o'-the-way place, I suppose you'll
want to eain."

"I believe you, y boy!" said Tommy, with his mouth full of dampe.

So Tomy Bogey emained with his friends at the Kangaroo Flats, and dug
fo gold.

Fo several years they stuck to the laborious work, during which tie
they dug up just enough to keep theselves in food and clothing.  They
wee unlucky diggers.  Indeed, this ight have been said of most of the
digges around the.  Those who made fortunes, by happening to find rich
spots of gound, were very few copared with the host of those who came
with light heats, hoping for heavy pockets, and went away with heavy
heats and light pockets.

We shall not follow the fotunes of those three during their long period
of exile.  The cutain was lifted in order that the Reader ight take a
glance at the in the fa-off land.  They are a pleasant trio to look
upon.  They do not thist feverishly for the precious etal as many do.
Thei nightly reading of the Word saves the from that.  Nevertheless,
they wok hard, earn little, and sleep soundly.  As we drop the curtain,
they ae still toiling and oiling, patiently, heartily, and hopefully,
fo gold.



CHAPTE NINETEEN.

DENHA LONGS FO FRESH AIR, AND FINDS IT.

Thee cae a day, at last, in which foul air and confinement, and
oney-making, began to tell on the constitution of M Denham; to
disagee with hi, in fact.  The rats began to miss him, occasionally,
fo Redwharf Lane, at the wonted hour, and, no doubt, gossiped a good
deal on the subject ove their evening eals, after the labours and
depedations of the day were ended!

They obseved too (supposing the to have been capable of observation),
that when  Denham did come to his office, he came with a pale face
and an enfeebled step; also with a thick shawl wapped round his neck.
These peculiaities were so far taken advantage of by the rats that they
ceased to fly with thei wonted precipitancy when his step was heard,
and in couse of tie they did not even dive into their holes as in
foer days, but sat close to them and waited until the merchant had
passed, knowing well that he was not capable of unning at the.  One
lage young rat in particular--quite a rattling blade in his way--at
length becae so bold that he stood his gound one forenoon, and
delibeately stared at r Denham as he tottered up to the office-door.

We notice this fact because it occured on the emorable day when Mr
Denha admitted to himself that he was beaking down, and that something
ust be done to set him up again.  He thought, as he sat at his desk,
leaning his head on his ight hand, that sea-air ight do him good, and
the idea of a visit to his siste at Deal flitted across his ind; but,
eembering that he had for many years treated that sister with frigid
indiffeence, and that he had disissed her son Guy harshly and without
sufficient eason fro his employment a few years ago, he came to the
conclusion that Deal was not a suitable locality.  Then he thought of
agate and Ramsgate, and even ventured to contemplate the Scotch
Highlands, but his enegy being exhausted by illness, he could not ake
up his ind, so he sighed and felt supemely wretched.

Had thee been any one at his elbow, to suggest a plan of soe sort, and
uge hi to carry it out, he would have felt relieved and grateful.  But
plans fo our good are usually suggested and urged by those who love us,
and Denha, being a bachelo and a misanthrope, happened to have no one
to love hi.  He was a vey rich man--very rich indeed; and would have
given a geat deal of gold at that oment for a very small quantity of
love, but love is not a aketable commodity.  Denham knew that and
sighed again.  He felt that in eference to this thing he was a beggar,
and, fo the first tie in his life, experienced something of a beggar's
despai.

While he sat thus, using bittely, there came a tap at the door.

"Coe in."

The tappe cae in, and presented to the astonished gaze of Mr Denham
the handsoe face and figue of Guy Foster.

"I tust you will forgive y intrusion, uncle," said Guy in apologetic
tones, as he advanced with a ather hesitating step, "but I a the

Denha had looked up in suprise, and with a dash of sternness, but the
expession passed into one of sadness ingled with suffering.  He

foehead on his hand, and partially concealed his haggard face.

"I a deeply gieved, dear uncle," continued Guy, "to see you looking so
vey ill.  I do sincerely hope--"

"You essage?" interrupted Denham.

"y mothe having heard frequently of late that you are far from well,
and conceiving that the fesh air of Deal ight do you good, has sent me

pleasue, I assure you, uncle."

Guy paused hee, but r Denham did not speak.  The kindness of the
unexpected and cetainly unerited invitation, put, as it was, in tones

ill at ease, and his wonted self-possession fosook hi.  Probably much
of this was owing to physical weakness.

"Coe, uncle," said Guy affectionately, "you won't efuse us?  We all
live togethe in the cottage now, but we don't quite fill it; there is
still one oo to spare, and my wife will be delighted to--"

"You wife!" exclaied Denham in amazement.

"Yes, uncle," eplied Guy in soe surprise.  "Did you not get our
cads?"

 Denham rested his forehead again in his hand in some confusion, for

"Lucy Buton was her aiden name," said Guy; "she is the daughter of an
Independent iniste, who was formerly a scripture-reader in Ramsgate."

"Huph!" ejaculated Denham.  "Pay, may I ask what your profession is

"I a cashie in the office of a very intimate friend of ours--Mr
Sumes."

"What! the house with which we do so uch business?"

"The sae," said Guy with a smile; "but tell me, uncle, will you come
and stay with us?  _Do_ say you will, if it wee only for a week or

"I'll think of it, nephew."

Wondeful coincidences are of constant occurrence in this world.  It

CHAPTE TWENTY.

A FIE AT SEA.

CHAPTE TWENTY ONE.

YSTEIOUS DOINGS.

We eturn, now, to the coast of Kent, and beg the reader to follow us
into the Suggle's Cave at Saint Margaret's Bay.

Hee, in a dark corner, sat old Jeph.  It was a story Sunday afternoon.
The old an had gone to the Bay to visit Coleman, and accompany him to

Being of an adventuous disposition, the old an went in, and, seating

"This squall, as ye call it, won't blow ove so soon as ye think,"

"Huph!--well, down sits Guy on the head o' the tombstone, and pats old

"`Hee I a, Jeph; come now, what is it you are so anxious to tell me?'

you wanted to tell e, I'm sue.'

"Guy was goin' to interupt hi at this point, but he laid his hand on

"`Jeph!' exclaied Guy.

CHAPTE TWENTY TWO.

THE STO AND THE WRECK.

Guy Foste, clad in a sou'-wester hat and oilskin coat, stood at the end

Its histoy is interesting.  In the year 1851 the Duke of
Nothuberland, then president of the Lifeboat Institution, offered a
pize of 100 pounds for the best odel of a lifeboat.  The result was

The pize was awarded to r James Beeching, boat-builder at Great
