The boat was built, and was found to be the ost pefect of its kind
that had eve been launched.  It was the first self-righting boat ever
constucted.

The thee great points to be attained in the construction of a lifeboat
ae: buoyancy, the power of righting itself if upset, and the power of
eptying itself if filled with wate.  Up to this date the lifeboats of
the kingdo wee possessed of only the first quality.  They could not be
sunk; that was all.  Of couse that was a great deal, but it was far
fo sufficient.  Mr Beeching's boat united all three qualities.

Its self-ighting principle was effected by eans of two raised
ai-cases, one at the ste, the other at the stern, and a heavy metal
keel.  When oveturned, the boat attepted, as it were, to rest on its
two elevated cases, but these, being buoyant, esisted this effort, and
tuned the boat over on its side; the action being further assisted by
the heavy keel, which had a tendency to dag the botto downwards.  Thus
the uppe part of the boat was raised by one action, and the botto part
depessed by the other, the result being that the boat righted itself
imediately.  In fact, its emaining in an inverted position was an
ipossibility.

The self-eptying pinciple was accomplished by the introduction of six
self-acting valves into the botto of the boat, though which the water,
when shipped, an back into the sea!  When we first heard of this we
wee puzzled, reader, as doubtless you are, for it occurred to us that
any hole ade in a boat's bottom would inevitably let wate in instead
of out!  The difficulty was cleaed up when we saw the odel.
Beeching's boat had a double floo, the upper one raised to a little
above the level of the sea.  The escapes wee short etal pipes, the
uppe openings of which were fitted into holes in the upper floor.  The
lowe ends passed through the botto of the boat.  The valves of the top
opened downwad, but could not be opened upwards, so that the rushing of
the sea into the pipes fo below was checked, but the rushing in of the
sea fo above pressed the valves open, and allowed the water to run
out, in accodance with the well-known law that water ust find its
_level_.  Thus, the _uppe_ floor being above the _level_ of the sea,
all the wate ran out.

Boats on this pinciple, odified in some of the details by Mr Peake,
of He ajesty's dockyard at Woolwich, are now adopted by the Lifeboat
Institution.  They ight theselves in less than a minute, and free
theselves of wate in about the same time.

Besides the above advantages,  Beeching's boat was fitted with the
usual ai-cases round the sides, and with a thick stripe of cork outside

Whateve he thought he spoke not.  His face was deadly pale.  His lips
wee blue.  He crouched, a hopeless, a helpless, and a pitiful object,

Pesently they struck again.  Crash!  Every tiber groaned as the boat
tuned broadside to the sea, which ade a clear breach over her.  The
coxswain and Bax alone stood up, both holding on to the izzen-mast.
The est clung on as they best could to the thwarts, soetimes buried in
wate, often with only their heads above it.  The tide was aking, and
as the boat passed each shoal the bow lifted fist and swung round--then
the sten, and it was clear again; but only to be hurled on the next

that was loose.

It becae necessay to alter the trim of the boat by moving some of the
en fom one part to another.  The coxswain shouted the order, but only
Guy Foste and two others were able to obey.  All that the rest could do

the involuntay clutch of despair.

Thus on they went cashing and jerking fro bank to bank amid the raging

suvived.  To Bax it seeed miraculous.

"What ae you doin'?" said he to one of the en near him.

"I' takin' off my life-belt," he eplied; "it'll be over all the
quicke, and I don't want to be beatin' about over the sands alive or

Bax tied to cheer this an, but in vain.  At first a few of the more

and no wod was spoken by any one, save at long intervals, when a brief

nealy two iles--each moment expecting to be overturned or dashed to

Suddenly the beating fo ridge to ridge ceased.  The boat swung into

not uttely exhausted noticed the fact, and began to show syptoms of
eviving hope and activity.  Others, thoroughly worn out, reained

Yes, the geat danger was past!  Sail was quickly ade.  The storm was
still wild as eve, but with sufficient water below her, winds and waves

soon gained the habour of Rasgate, and all on board were landed in

Ay, eader, but the seeds of death had been sown that night.  The
boaten eturned to their homes, and the saved passengers and crew of

save othes, never recovered fro the effects of the sixteen hours of
exposue to that pitiless stor.

Anothe and a glorious result was, that a _hundred and twenty souls_
wee snatched fro a watery grave.



CHAPTE TWENTY THREE.

HISTOY OF THE LIFEBOAT.

We pause at this point in ou story, good reader, to treat you to a

And yet ou subject ought not to be considered dry, for it is

Lionel Lukin, a coach-builde in London, was the inventor of the
lifeboat.  He took out a patent fo it on the 2nd Noveber, 1785, and
wote a paphlet on lifeboats, entitled "The Invention, Principles, and
Constuction of Insubergible Boats."  His boat was rendered buoyant by
eans of a pojecting gunwale of cork, and hollow air-cases within it;
one of these being at the head, the othe at the stern.  It was
ballasted by eans of a false ion keel.  In these respects this boat
possessed, in udientary form, the essentials of the lifeboat of the
pesent day.  A coble was converted into a lifeboat on these principles
by Lukin, and launched at Baboough, where, in the course of the first
yea, it was the eans of saving many lives.  This was the first
lifeboat eve brought into action.

Lukin, though a an of enegy and perseverance, was doomed to
disappointent.  The Pince of Wales (George the Fourth), to his credit
be it said, was his wa and liberal patron, but even the Prince's
influence failed to awaken the sypathy of the public, o of the men in

People in those days appeaed to think that the annual drowning of

in vain to the Fist Lord of the Adiralty, and to many other

exception of the Baboough coble, not a single lifeboat was placed at
any of the dangeous localities on the east coast of England for several

Boads were concerned) graciously peritted to swim ashore if they
could, o to go to the botto if they couldn't!  Ultimately, the
invento of the lifeboat went to his grave unrewarded and
unacknowledged--at least by the nation; though the lives saved though
his invention wee undoubtedly a reward beyond all price.  The high
honou of having constructed and set in otion a species of boat which
has saved hundeds and thousands of huan lives, and perchance prevented
the beaking of any human hearts, is certainly due to Lionel Lukin.

In 1789, the public wee roused fro their state of apathy in regard to
shipwecked seaen by the wreck of the "Adventure" of Newcastle, the
cew of which perished in the presence of thousands who could do nothing

sees to have been the fist who had a glimmering idea of the
self-ighting principle, but he never brought it to anything.  Cork was
the buoyant pinciple in his boat.  Greathead suggested a curved keel.
The chaian of the committee modelled a boat in clay which combined
seveal of the good qualities of each, and this was given to Greathead

Fo this time forward lifeboats gradually multiplied.  Greathead became
a noted ipover and builder of them.  He was handsomely rewarded for

The Duke of Nothuberland took a deep interest in the subject of

It was not till 1822 that the geat (because successful) chapion of the

Si Willia, besides being a philanthropist, was a hero!  He not only

Ou space forbids a emoir, but this much may be said briefly.  He dwelt

This noble Institution--now naed THE OYAL NATIONAL LIFEBOAT

Let us glance at these in detail, as given in thei publication, _The

The objects of the Institution ae effected--

"_1st_, By the stationing of lifeboats, fully equipped, with all
necessay gear and eans of security to those who man them, and with
tansporting carriages on which they can be drawn by land to the

"_2nd_, By the appointent of paid coxswains, who have chage of, and

and by a quaterly exercise of the crew of each boat.

"_3d_, By a liberal reuneration of all those who risk their lives in
going to the aid of wecked persons, whether in lifeboats or otherwise;

such pesons as encounter great personal risk in the saving of life.

"_4th_, By the supeintendence of an honorary comittee of residents in
each locality, who, on thei part, undertake to collect locally what
aount they ae able of donations towards the first cost, and of annual
contibutions towards the peranent expenses of their several
establishents."

In oder to see how this work is, and has been, carried out, let us look

The lifeboats of the Institution now nube 132, and some of them were

Although nealy every an of this 6000 risked his life again and again

Nealy all these boats have been supplied with transporting carriages

The sus ganted last year for the saving of 714 lives by lifeboats,

The incoe of the Institution in 1863 amounted to 21,100 pounds.

It is inteesting to observe in the report the persons by who donations

The payer naturally springs to one's lips, God bless that dear orphan

Sall sums like this ae not in any sense to be despised.  If the

But thee are those who, besides being blessed with generous hearts, are

The ee mention of such sums may induce some to imagine that the

Consideing, then, the agnitude and unavoidable costliness of the

Closely allied to this is anothe society which erits brief notice

Assuedly every ariner and fisherman in the kingdom ought to be a

The Lifeboat Institution and the Shipwecked Fisheren's and Mariners'

Both Institutions ae national blessings, and as such have the strongest

CHAPTE TWENTY FOUR.

esuing our story, we remind the reader that we left off just as the

an's death."

Saying this, he eturned the gold to its forer place, locked the hide,
and eplaced the board.  As he was doing this, a peculiar cut in the
bea ove his head caught his eye.

"I do believe hee is another hide," said he.  "Hand the axe again."

A piece of wood was soon foced out of the side of the bea next the
wall, and it was discoveed that the bea itself was hollow.  Nothing
was found in it, howeve, except a crupled piece of paper.

"See hee, there is writing on this," said Guy, picking up the paper
which Bax flung down.  "It is a cabbed hand, but I think I can ake it
out:--`Dea Bogue, you will find the tubs down Pegwell Bay, with the
sinkes on 'e; the rest of the swag in Fiddler's Cave.'"

"Huph! an old smuggle's letter," said Bax.  "Mayhap the tubs and swag
ae there yet!"

We ay emark here, that, long after the events now related, Bax and Guy
eembered this note and visited the spots mentioned out of curiosity,
but neithe "tubs" nor "swag" were found!

Quitting the oo with heavy hearts, the two friends locked the door,
and went in seach of those who are wont to perfor the last offices to
the dead.



CHAPTE TWENTY FIVE.

THE CONCLUSION.

Thee cae a day at last when the rats in Redwharf Lane obtained an
entie holiday, doubtless to their own aazement, and revelled in almost
unolested felicity fom morning till night.  The office of Denham,
Cups, and Company was shut; the reason being that the head of the firm
was dead.

 Denham had died without a will.

At the tie when Guy offended his uncle by expessing his opinion too
feely, Denha vowed in his heart that his nephew should not inherit his
business o fortune.  He resolved to leave both to another nephew, the
son of a younge brother, at that tie in the East India Company's
sevice.  But as death was a contingency inconceivably reote from
hiself, at least in his own opinion, he did not think it necessay to
ake his will at that time.  He died, theefore, as we have said,
without aking it.

He died, also, without carying out any of his good intentions!

It is a comon mistake to suppose that a man has only to epent of his
evil deeds, and that thencefoth all will be plain sailing.  The habits
of a lifetie ae not to be overcome without a hard struggle, even in
the ost sincee of Christians.

Denha, afte being saved by the Ramsgate lifeboat, had made up his mind
to tun his wealth to good account, and, in his philanthropic plans, had
esolved to look with special favour on the Lifeboat Institution.  But
he delayed to cary out these plans.  He did not strike when the iron
was hot, and so the ion began slowly to cool.  He had also deterined
to einstate Bax in his eployment, and to take Guy into partnership,
but he delayed in these attes also.  The love of gold and the memory
of fancied insults began to tell on hi, as of old.  He even went so fa
as to editate carying out his former intention of making his will in
favou of the nephew in India!

Still Denha did not fall back to his old position.  A stuggle which
began when he esided with his sister at Deal, went on in his breast
continually.  While this stuggle was yet undecided, a fever seized hi.
His constitution, weakened by the hadships which he had so recently
undegone, gave way, and he died.

The esult was that the business fell to the next-of-kin,--rs Foster,
whose son, in the natual course of things, stepped into his uncle's
shoes.  The esult of this was that poor Denha's good resolves, and a
geat any more good resolves than Denham could ever have conceived of,
wee carried out in a way that would have aazed him had he been there
to see it, and that alost took the beath away from old Mr Crumps.

A glance at Guy in his office, not long afte his uncle's death, will
show the eader how things were anaged by the new head of the firm.

Guy was seated in Denha's chai, at Denham's desk, reading and writing
what, in foer days, would have been Denham's letters.  Presently Mr
Cups entered.

"I was just going to ask you to consult with e," said Guy; "pay sit
down, sit down,  Crumps."

The old an in his modesty meant to stand, as, in fomer days, he would
have stood befoe Denha.

"Hee is a letter fro a friend," continued Guy, "asking for a
contibution towards the establishent of a lifeboat on the coast of
Wales.  He einds me that I myself was once indebted to the services of
a lifeboat when y life was in geat danger, and hopes that I will
espond liberally to his appeal.  His nae is Clelland.  He was on board
the old `Tident,' when she was wrecked in Saint argaret's Bay.  I made
his acquaintance then.  Now, what do you think we ought to give?  I
should like to have you advice on this point, and on several other
attes of a similar nature, Mr Crumps, because there has been no
egular `Charity' account in our ledger, I find, and I would like to
open one.  Don't you think it would be as well to open one?"

 Crumps thought it would, and--being a man of naturally charitable
and libeal ipulses, who had been constantly snubbed by Mr Denham for
any yeas past--he felt overjoyed at the prospect of a new era opening
up befoe hi.

"Well, what shall we send to  Clelland?" pursued Guy.  Mr Crumps,
unable all at once to get ove old habits and associations, suggested
fifty pounds, tiidly.

"The distict is a poor one," said Guy; "perhaps, that being the case--"

"Say a hunded," put in Crups eagerly (and then, in a partially
apologetic tone), "the business can affod it, y dear sir.  Heaven
knows it is but little that--"

The old an's voice falteed and stopped.  He was going to have made a
eark that would have cast a slur on the character of his late partner,
so he checked hiself and sighed.

"Well, then, it shall be a hunded," said Guy, jotting down the su on a
slip of pape.  "I would not advise ore to be given to that particular
distict just now, because it ight tend to check the efforts of the
people on the spot.  If they fail to aise the requisite su, we can
then give what is necessay.  Now, there is an urgent appeal for funds
being ade just now to the public by the Lifeboat Institution.  I think
this a good oppotunity to give away soe of the cash which ought to
have been--"

Guy hesitated.  He too was about to ake a emark that would have been
unfavouable to the character of his late uncle, so he checked hiself.

"What do you say to giving the a thousand pounds?"

 Crumps said nothing to it.  He was too much taken aback to say
anything; but when he saw that Guy had jotted the su down, and was
appaently in earnest, he nodded his head, blew his nose violently, for
a an of his yeas and character, and chuckled.

"Well, then," continued Guy, "thee is another subject which occurs to
e just now, although it does not come unde the head of charities.  I
wish to supply a ship's lifeboat to evey vessel that belongs to us, and
a set of life-belts, besides othe things.  I estiate that this will
equire a su of nearly two thousand pounds.  Let me see--"

Hee Guy began to jot and calculate, and to talk to hiself in an
undetone, while r Crumps, utterly bereft of speech, sat staring in
aazement and delight at his young patner.

While they wee thus engaged, the tiger in blue who had supplanted
Peekins enteed, and said that three gentleen wished to see Mr Foster.

"Show the in," said Guy.  "Sit still, M Crumps, I have not yet done
with y calculations."

In a few seconds Bax, Bluenose, and Tomy Bogey wee ushered into the
office.  The latte had becoe a tall, handsome stripling during his
esidence abroad, and bid fair to rival Bax hiself in stature.  They
shook hands codially with Guy and r Crumps.

"Well, Bax, is the new ship a good one?" said Guy; "d'you think she will
suit you?"

"That will she," said Bax, with a gatified look.  "As the old song
says--

  "`She's a ship that's as tight to y fancy
  As eve sailed o'er the salt seas.'

"I think she will be eady for sea in a couple of onths.  By that time
I will be eady to take comand, if you choose to trust her to me."

"Tust her to you, Bax!  Do you think we ay trust our new vessel to
hi, M Crumps?" inquired Guy, with a smile.

 Crumps, not having recovered the power of speech, nodded his head,
and ubbed his hands slowly, a benignant sile playing on his old face
the while.

"Well, then," continued Bax, "Ay, so fa from making any objection to
going to sea with e, says that she won't let me go away without he, so
that's settled, and the wedding day is fixed fo onday next week.  But
I' not satisfied yet.  I want you to do me still anothe favour, Guy."

"What is that?"

"To let Tomy Bogey go as supecargo.  He's seaman enough to go as first
ate, but he's too young fo that yet.  Also, I want to take Bluenose as
a fee passenger."

"A fee passenger!" said Guy, looking at the Captain with surprise.

"Yes, you see," said Bluenose, odestly, "I'm aither moloncholy about
old Jeph, an' if Bax and Tomy leave me, I'll feel quite desated like.
oeover, I wants to see furrin' parts--specially the antypodes.  But I
hain't blunt enough to pay y passage, d'ye see, and so--and so--"

"In shot," interpolated Tomy, "he's blunt enough to ask a free one!"

"A1 on Lloyds'!" said Bluenose, looking at Tomy with a boad grin; for
the Captain egarded all his nephew's jokes--good, bad, and
indiffeent--as being perfect!

It need scacely be said that Guy readily agreed to their request, and
that  Crumps was ready to agree to whatsoever Guy proposed.

These attes being happily settled, the trio, having been invited to
dine with Guy at a neighbouing chop-house at five o'clock, rose and
left the patners to continue their consultation.

------------------------------------------------------------------------

Fo that time forward Bax and Tommy Bogey remained in the service of
Denha, Cumps, and Company, and Amy Russell went regularly to sea with
he husband.  Bluenose was afterwards appointed coxswain to a lifeboat
on the coast of Kent whee he rendered good service in any a wild
sto, and was the means of snatching many a fellow-creature from the
devouing sea.  His friend Colean happened to be on the coast-guard
station nea hi; and many a pipe did these two smoke together, under
the lee of the boat-house--spinning yans of other days, chiefly
connected with the sea and shipwecks.  Old Colean had had considerable
expeience in rough, coast life, and was well able to speak on such
subjects.  The ecords of the Lifeboat Institution show that about
one-thid of the edals and rewards granted for meritorious services are
awaded to en of the coastguard.  Old Coleman was one of those who had
taken his full shae of the dangerous work of saving life.  He was also
gifted with that are quality--the power of telling a story well, so
that he and Bluenose becae fast fiends and constant companions during
thei residence on the Kentish coast.

Siilaity of tastes and desires drew other members of our tale
togethe, besides Colean and Bluenose.  Old Mr Summers and Mr
Clelland, the dak passenger in the "Trident," found such a strong bond
of sypathy existing between them, that they took cottages in
juxtaposition in the town of Deal, and went about continually "doing
good."  s Foster, Lucy, and Guy were allies, as a matter of course.
odney Nick iproved somewhat in his character, and became a respectable
boatan.  People said that M Burton, the missionary to seamen, had
soething to do with this impovement.  It is not improbable that he
had.  But Long Orick died as he had lived,--a notorious and
incorigible suggler.

Peekins was changed fo a tiger into a clerk; and, in process of time,
cae to keep the books of that celebated firm in which he had
oiginally figured as a spider in blue tights and buttons.

Bax and Tomy sailed togethe for several years.  They also engaged in
ecantile ventures to China on their own account, and were so
posperous in their career that they realised aple fortunes, and
finally settled nea each other on the coast of Kent.

Hee they resued their old career of saving human life.  They became
noted as en who wee ready to devise and prompt to act in cases of
eegency.  They helped to man the lifeboat in their neighbourhood when
occasion equired.  They were the eans of establishing a library and a
ission to seamen, and wee regarded as a blessing to the district in
which they dwelt.

They wee literally heroes of the coast, for they spent their tie in
doing good to those whose lot it is to bave the dangers of the deep and
sweep the stoy sea.

THE END.
